6543	he remembered how once after the explosion of a rocket bomb he had helped to drag a corpse out of some ruins and had been astonished not only by the incredible weight of the thing but by its rigidity and awkwardness to handle which made it seem more like stone than flesh
6544	her body felt like that
6545	it occurred to him that the texture of her skin would be quite different from what it had once been
6546	he did not attempt to kiss her nor did they speak
6547	as they walked back across the grass she looked directly at him for the first time
6548	it was only a momentary glance full of contempt and dislike
6549	he wondered whether it was a dislike that came purely out of the past or whether it was inspired also by his bloated face and the water that the wind kept squeezing from his eyes
6550	they sat down on two iron chairs side by side but not too close together
6551	he saw that she was about to speak
6552	she moved her clumsy shoe a few centimetres and deliberately crushed a twig
6553	her feet seemed to have grown broader he noticed
6554	i betrayed you she said baldly
6555	i betrayed you he said
6556	she gave him another quick look of dislike
6557	sometimes she said they threaten you with something something you cant stand up to cant even think about
6558	and then you say dont do it to me do it to somebody else do it to soandso
6559	and perhaps you might pretend afterwards that it was only a trick and that you just said it to make them stop and didnt really mean it
6560	but that isnt true
6561	at the time when it happens you do mean it
6562	you think theres no other way of saving yourself and youre quite ready to save yourself that way
6563	you want it to happen to the other person
6564	you dont give a damn what they suffer
6565	all you care about is yourself
6566	all you care about is yourself he echoed
6567	and after that you dont feel the same towards the other person any longer
6568	no he said you dont feel the same
6569	there did not seem to be anything more to say
6570	the wind plastered their thin overalls against their bodies
6571	almost at once it became embarrassing to sit there in silence besides it was too cold to keep still
6572	she said something about catching her tube and stood up to go
6573	we must meet again he said
6574	yes she said we must meet again
6575	he followed irresolutely for a little distance half a pace behind her
6576	they did not speak again
6577	she did not actually try to shake him off but walked at just such a speed as to prevent his keeping abreast of her
6578	he had made up his mind that he would accompany her as far as the tube station but suddenly this process of trailing along in the cold seemed pointless and unbearable
6579	he was overwhelmed by a desire not so much to get away from julia as to get back to the chestnut tree cafe which had never seemed so attractive as at this moment
6580	he had a nostalgic vision of his corner table with the newspaper and the chessboard and the everflowing gin
6581	above all it would be warm in there
6582	the next moment not altogether by accident he allowed himself to become separated from her by a small knot of people
6583	he made a halfhearted attempt to catch up then slowed down turned and made off in the opposite direction
6584	when he had gone fifty metres he looked back
6585	the street was not crowded but already he could not distinguish her
6586	any one of a dozen hurrying figures might have been hers
6587	perhaps her thickened stiffened body was no longer recognizable from behind
6588	at the time when it happens she had said you do mean it
6589	he had meant it
6590	he had not merely said it he had wished it
6591	he had wished that she and not he should be delivered over to the
6592	something changed in the music that trickled from the telescreen
6593	a cracked and jeering note a yellow note came into it
6594	and thenperhaps it was not happening perhaps it was only a memory taking on the semblance of sounda voice was singing
6595	under the spreading chestnut tree
6596	i sold you and you sold me
6597	the tears welled up in his eyes
6598	a passing waiter noticed that his glass was empty and came back with the gin bottle
6599	he took up his glass and sniffed at it
6600	the stuff grew not less but more horrible with every mouthful he drank
6601	but it had become the element he swam in
6602	it was his life his death and his resurrection
6603	it was gin that sank him into stupor every night and gin that revived him every morning
6604	when he woke seldom before eleven hundred with gummedup eyelids and fiery mouth and a back that seemed to be broken it would have been impossible even to rise from the horizontal if it had not been for the bottle and teacup placed beside the bed overnight
6605	through the midday hours he sat with glazed face the bottle handy listening to the telescreen
6606	from fifteen to closingtime he was a fixture in the chestnut tree
6607	no one cared what he did any longer no whistle woke him no telescreen admonished him
6608	occasionally perhaps twice a week he went to a dusty forgottenlooking office in the ministry of truth and did a little work or what was called work
6609	he had been appointed to a subcommittee of a subcommittee which had sprouted from one of the innumerable committees dealing with minor difficulties that arose in the compilation of the eleventh edition of the newspeak dictionary
6610	they were engaged in producing something called an interim report but what it was that they were reporting on he had never definitely found out
6611	it was something to do with the question of whether commas should be placed inside brackets or outside
6612	there were four others on the committee all of them persons similar to himself
6613	there were days when they assembled and then promptly dispersed again frankly admitting to one another that there was not really anything to be done
6614	but there were other days when they settled down to their work almost eagerly making a tremendous show of entering up their minutes and drafting long memoranda which were never finishedwhen the argument as to what they were supposedly arguing about grew extraordinarily involved and abstruse with subtle haggling over definitions enormous digressions quarrelsthreats even to appeal to higher authority
6615	and then suddenly the life would go out of them and they would sit round the table looking at one another with extinct eyes like ghosts fading at cockcrow
6616	the telescreen was silent for a moment
6617	winston raised his head again
6618	the bulletin
6619	but no they were merely changing the music
6620	he had the map of africa behind his eyelids
6621	the movement of the armies was a diagram a black arrow tearing vertically southward and a white arrow horizontally eastward across the tail of the first
6622	as though for reassurance he looked up at the imperturbable face in the portrait
6623	was it conceivable that the second arrow did not even exist
6624	his interest flagged again
6625	he drank another mouthful of gin picked up the white knight and made a tentative move
6626	check
6627	but it was evidently not the right move because
6628	uncalled a memory floated into his mind

6630	his mother was sitting opposite him and also laughing
6631	it must have been about a month before she disappeared
6632	it was a moment of reconciliation when the nagging hunger in his belly was forgotten and his earlier affection for her had temporarily revived
6633	he remembered the day well a pelting drenching day when the water streamed down the windowpane and the light indoors was too dull to read by
6634	the boredom of the two children in the dark cramped bedroom became unbearable
6635	winston whined and grizzled made futile demands for food fretted about the room pulling everything out of place and kicking the wainscoting until the neighbours banged on the wall while the younger child wailed intermittently
6636	in the end his mother said now be good and ill buy you a toy
6637	a lovely toyyoull love it and then she had gone out in the rain to a little general shop which was still sporadically open nearby and came back with a cardboard box containing an outfit of snakes and ladders
6638	he could still remember the smell of the damp cardboard
6639	it was a miserable outfit
6640	the board was cracked and the tiny wooden dice were so illcut that they would hardly lie on their sides
6641	winston looked at the thing sulkily and without interest
6642	but then his mother lit a piece of candle and they sat down on the floor to play
6643	soon he was wildly excited and shouting with laughter as the tiddlywinks climbed hopefully up the ladders and then came slithering down the snakes again almost to the startingpoint
6644	they played eight games winning four each

6646	for a whole afternoon they had all been happy together as in his earlier childhood
6647	he pushed the picture out of his mind
6648	it was a false memory
6649	he was troubled by false memories occasionally

6681	he was walking down the whitetiled corridor with the feeling of walking in sunlight and an armed guard at his back
6682	the longhopedfor bullet was entering his brain

6692	appendix
6693	the principles of newspeak
6694	newspeak was the official language of oceania and had been devised to meet the ideological needs of ingsoc or english socialism
6695	in the year there was not as yet anyone who used newspeak as his sole means of communication either in speech or writing
6696	the leading articles in the times were written in it but this was a tour de force which could only be carried out by a specialist
6697	it was expected that newspeak would have finally superseded oldspeak or standard english as we should call it by about the year
6698	meanwhile it gained ground steadily all party members tending to use newspeak words and grammatical constructions more and more in their everyday speech
6699	the version in use in and embodied in the ninth and tenth editions of the newspeak dictionary was a provisional one and contained many superfluous words and archaic formations which were due to be suppressed later
6700	it is with the final perfected version as embodied in the eleventh edition of the dictionary that we are concerned here
6701	the purpose of newspeak was not only to provide a medium of expression for the worldview and mental habits proper to the devotees of ingsoc but to make all other modes of thought impossible
6702	it was intended that when newspeak had been adopted once and for all and oldspeak forgotten a heretical thoughtthat is a thought diverging from the principles of ingsocshould be literally unthinkable at least so far as thought is dependent on words
6703	its vocabulary was so constructed as to give exact and often very subtle expression to every meaning that a party member could properly wish to express while excluding all other meanings and also the possibility of arriving at them by indirect methods
6704	this was done partly by the invention of new words but chiefly by eliminating undesirable words and by stripping such words as remained of unorthodox meanings and so far as possible of all secondary meanings whatever
6705	to give a single example
6706	the word free still existed in newspeak but it could only be used in such statements as this dog is free from lice or this field is free from weeds
6707	it could not be used in its old sense of politically free or intellectually free since political and intellectual freedom no longer existed even as concepts and were therefore of necessity nameless
6708	quite apart from the suppression of definitely heretical words reduction of vocabulary was regarded as an end in itself and no word that could be dispensed with was allowed to survive
6709	newspeak was designed not to extend but to diminish the range of thought and this purpose was indirectly assisted by cutting the choice of words down to a minimum
6710	newspeak was founded on the english language as we now know it though many newspeak sentences even when not containing newlycreated words would be barely intelligible to an englishspeaker of our own day
6711	newspeak words were divided into three distinct classes known as the a vocabulary the b vocabulary also called compound words and the c vocabulary
6712	it will be simpler to discuss each class separately but the grammatical peculiarities of the language can be dealt with in the section devoted to the a vocabulary since the same rules held good for all three categories
6713	the a vocabulary
6714	the a vocabulary consisted of the words needed for the business of everyday lifefor such things as eating drinking working putting on ones clothes going up and down stairs riding in vehicles gardening cooking and the like
6715	it was composed almost entirely of words that we already possess words like hit run dog tree sugar house fieldbut in comparison with the presentday english vocabulary their number was extremely small while their meanings were far more rigidly defined
6716	all ambiguities and shades of meaning had been purged out of them
6717	so far as it could be achieved a newspeak word of this class was simply a staccato sound expressing one clearly understood concept
6718	it would have been quite impossible to use the a vocabulary for literary purposes or for political or philosophical discussion
6719	it was intended only to express simple purposive thoughts usually involving concrete objects or physical actions
6720	the grammar of newspeak had two outstanding peculiarities
6721	the first of these was an almost complete interchangeability between different parts of speech
6722	any word in the language in principle this applied even to very abstract words such as if or when could be used either as verb noun adjective or adverb
6723	between the verb and the noun form when they were of the same root there was never any variation this rule of itself involving the destruction of many archaic forms
6724	the word thought for example did not exist in newspeak
6725	its place was taken by think which did duty for both noun and verb
6726	no etymological principle was followed here in some cases it was the original noun that was chosen for retention in other cases the verb
6727	even where a noun and verb of kindred meaning were not etymologically connected one or other of them was frequently suppressed
6728	there was for example no such word as cut its meaning being sufficiently covered by the nounverb knife
6729	adjectives were formed by adding the suffix ful to the nounverb and adverbs by adding wise
6730	thus for example speedful meant rapid and speedwise meant quickly
6731	certain of our presentday adjectives such as good strong big black soft were retained but their total number was very small
6732	there was little need for them since almost any adjectival meaning could be arrived at by adding ful to a nounverb
6733	none of the nowexisting adverbs was retained except for a very few already ending in wise the wise termination was invariable
6734	the word well for example was replaced by goodwise
6735	in addition any wordthis again applied in principle to every word in the languagecould be negatived by adding the affix un or could be strengthened by the affix plus or for still greater emphasis doubleplus
6736	thus for example uncold meant warm while pluscold and doublepluscold meant respectively very cold and superlatively cold
6737	it was also possible as in presentday english to modify the meaning of almost any word by prepositional affixes such as ante post up down etc
6738	by such methods it was found possible to bring about an enormous diminution of vocabulary
6739	given for instance the word good there was no need for such a word as bad since the required meaning was equally wellindeed betterexpressed by ungood
6740	all that was necessary in any case where two words formed a natural pair of opposites was to decide which of them to suppress
6741	dark for example could be replaced by unlight or light by undark according to preference
6742	the second distinguishing mark of newspeak grammar was its regularity
6743	subject to a few exceptions which are mentioned below all inflexions followed the same rules
6744	thus in all verbs the preterite and the past participle were the same and ended in ed
6745	the preterite of steal was stealed the preterite of think was thinked and so on throughout the language all such forms as swam gave brought spoke taken etc being abolished
6746	all plurals were made by adding s or es as the case might be
6747	the plurals of man ox life were mans oxes lifes
6748	comparison of adjectives was invariably made by adding er est good gooder goodest irregular forms and the more most formation being suppressed
6749	the only classes of words that were still allowed to inflect irregularly were the pronouns the relatives the demonstrative adjectives and the auxiliary verbs
6750	all of these followed their ancient usage except that whom had been scrapped as unnecessary and the shall should tenses had been dropped all their uses being covered by will and would
6751	there were also certain irregularities in wordformation arising out of the need for rapid and easy speech
6752	a word which was difficult to utter or was liable to be incorrectly heard was held to be ipso facto a bad word occasionally therefore for the sake of euphony extra letters were inserted into a word or an archaic formation was retained
6753	but this need made itself felt chiefly in connexion with the b vocabulary
6754	why so great an importance was attached to ease of pronunciation will be made clear later in this essay
6755	the b vocabulary
6756	the b vocabulary consisted of words which had been deliberately constructed for political purposes words that is to say which not only had in every case a political implication but were intended to impose a desirable mental attitude upon the person using them
6757	without a full understanding of the principles of ingsoc it was difficult to use these words correctly
6758	in some cases they could be translated into oldspeak or even into words taken from the a vocabulary but this usually demanded a long paraphrase and always involved the loss of certain overtones
6759	the b words were a sort of verbal shorthand often packing whole ranges of ideas into a few syllables and at the same time more accurate and forcible than ordinary language
6760	the b words were in all cases compound words
6761	compound words such as speakwrite were of course to be found in the a vocabulary but these were merely convenient abbreviations and had no special ideological colour
6762	they consisted of two or more words or portions of words welded together in an easily pronounceable form
6763	the resulting amalgam was always a nounverb and inflected according to the ordinary rules
6764	to take a single example the word goodthink meaning very roughly orthodoxy or if one chose to regard it as a verb to think in an orthodox manner
6765	this inflected as follows nounverb goodthink past tense and past participle goodthinked present participle goodthinking adjective goodthinkful adverb goodthinkwise verbal noun goodthinker
6766	the b words were not constructed on any etymological plan
6767	the words of which they were made up could be any parts of speech and could be placed in any order and mutilated in any way which made them easy to pronounce while indicating their derivation
6768	in the word crimethink thoughtcrime for instance the think came second whereas in thinkpol thought police it came first and in the latter word police had lost its second syllable
6769	because of the great difficulty in securing euphony irregular formations were commoner in the b vocabulary than in the a vocabulary
6770	for example the adjective forms of minitrue minipax and miniluv were respectively minitruthful minipeaceful and minilovely simply because trueful paxful and loveful were slightly awkward to pronounce
6771	in principle however all b words could inflect and all inflected in exactly the same way
6772	some of the b words had highly subtilized meanings barely intelligible to anyone who had not mastered the language as a whole
6773	consider for example such a typical sentence from a times leading article as oldthinkers unbellyfeel ingsoc
6774	the shortest rendering that one could make of this in oldspeak would be those whose ideas were formed before the revolution cannot have a full emotional understanding of the principles of english socialism
6775	but this is not an adequate translation
6776	to begin with in order to grasp the full meaning of the newspeak sentence quoted above one would have to have a clear idea of what is meant by ingsoc
6777	and in addition only a person thoroughly grounded in ingsoc could appreciate the full force of the word bellyfeel which implied a blind enthusiastic acceptance difficult to imagine today or of the word oldthink which was inextricably mixed up with the idea of wickedness and decadence
6778	but the special function of certain newspeak words of which oldthink was one was not so much to express meanings as to destroy them
6779	these words necessarily few in number had had their meanings extended until they contained within themselves whole batteries of words which as they were sufficiently covered by a single comprehensive term could now be scrapped and forgotten
6780	the greatest difficulty facing the compilers of the newspeak dictionary was not to invent new words but having invented them to make sure what they meant to make sure that is to say what ranges of words they cancelled by their existence
6781	as we have already seen in the case of the word free words which had once borne a heretical meaning were sometimes retained for the sake of convenience but only with the undesirable meanings purged out of them
6782	countless other words such as honour justice morality internationalism democracy science and religion had simply ceased to exist
6783	a few blanket words covered them and in covering them abolished them
6784	all words grouping themselves round the concepts of liberty and equality for instance were contained in the single word crimethink while all words grouping themselves round the concepts of objectivity and rationalism were contained in the single word oldthink
6785	greater precision would have been dangerous
6786	what was required in a party member was an outlook similar to that of the ancient hebrew who knew without knowing much else that all nations other than his own worshipped false gods
6787	he did not need to know that these gods were called baal osiris moloch ashtaroth and the like probably the less he knew about them the better for his orthodoxy
6788	he knew jehovah and the commandments of jehovah he knew therefore that all gods with other names or other attributes were false gods
6789	in somewhat the same way the party member knew what constituted right conduct and in exceedingly vague generalized terms he knew what kinds of departure from it were possible
6790	his sexual life for example was entirely regulated by the two newspeak words sexcrime sexual immorality and goodsex chastity
6791	sexcrime covered all sexual misdeeds whatever
6792	it covered fornication adultery homosexuality and other perversions and in addition normal intercourse practised for its own sake
6793	there was no need to enumerate them separately since they were all equally culpable and in principle all punishable by death
6794	in the c vocabulary which consisted of scientific and technical words it might be necessary to give specialized names to certain sexual aberrations but the ordinary citizen had no need of them
6795	he knew what was meant by goodsexthat is to say normal intercourse between man and wife for the sole purpose of begetting children and without physical pleasure on the part of the woman all else was sexcrime
6796	in newspeak it was seldom possible to follow a heretical thought further than the perception that it was heretical beyond that point the necessary words were nonexistent
6797	no word in the b vocabulary was ideologically neutral
6798	a great many were euphemisms
6799	such words for instance as joycamp forcedlabour camp or minipax ministry of peace ie
6800	ministry of war meant almost the exact opposite of what they appeared to mean
6801	some words on the other hand displayed a frank and contemptuous understanding of the real nature of oceanic society
6802	an example was prolefeed meaning the rubbishy entertainment and spurious news which the party handed out to the masses
6803	other words again were ambivalent having the connotation good when applied to the party and bad when applied to its enemies
6804	but in addition there were great numbers of words which at first sight appeared to be mere abbreviations and which derived their ideological colour not from their meaning but from their structure
6805	so far as it could be contrived everything that had or might have political significance of any kind was fitted into the b vocabulary
6806	the name of every organization or body of people or doctrine or country or institution or public building was invariably cut down into the familiar shape that is a single easily pronounced word with the smallest number of syllables that would preserve the original derivation
6807	in the ministry of truth for example the records department in which winston smith worked was called recdep the fiction department was called ficdep the teleprogrammes department was called teledep and so on
6808	this was not done solely with the object of saving time
6809	even in the early decades of the twentieth century telescoped words and phrases had been one of the characteristic features of political language and it had been noticed that the tendency to use abbreviations of this kind was most marked in totalitarian countries and totalitarian organizations
6810	examples were such words as nazi gestapo comintern inprecorr agitprop
6811	in the beginning the practice had been adopted as it were instinctively but in newspeak it was used with a conscious purpose
6812	it was perceived that in thus abbreviating a name one narrowed and subtly altered its meaning by cutting out most of the associations that would otherwise cling to it
6813	the words communist international for instance call up a composite picture of universal human brotherhood red flags barricades karl marx and the paris commune
6814	the word comintern on the other hand suggests merely a tightlyknit organization and a welldefined body of doctrine
6815	it refers to something almost as easily recognized and as limited in purpose as a chair or a table
6816	comintern is a word that can be uttered almost without taking thought whereas communist international is a phrase over which one is obliged to linger at least momentarily
6817	in the same way the associations called up by a word like minitrue are fewer and more controllable than those called up by ministry of truth
6818	this accounted not only for the habit of abbreviating whenever possible but also for the almost exaggerated care that was taken to make every word easily pronounceable
6819	in newspeak euphony outweighed every consideration other than exactitude of meaning
6820	regularity of grammar was always sacrificed to it when it seemed necessary
6821	and rightly so since what was required above all for political purposes was short clipped words of unmistakable meaning which could be uttered rapidly and which roused the minimum of echoes in the speakers mind
6822	the words of the b vocabulary even gained in force from the fact that nearly all of them were very much alike
6823	almost invariably these wordsgoodthink minipax prolefeed sexcrime joycamp ingsoc bellyfeel thinkpol and countless otherswere words of two or three syllables with the stress distributed equally between the first syllable and the last
6824	the use of them encouraged a gabbling style of speech at once staccato and monotonous
6825	and this was exactly what was aimed at
6826	the intention was to make speech and especially speech on any subject not ideologically neutral as nearly as possible independent of consciousness
6827	for the purposes of everyday life it was no doubt necessary or sometimes necessary to reflect before speaking but a party member called upon to make a political or ethical judgement should be able to spray forth the correct opinions as automatically as a machine gun spraying forth bullets
6828	his training fitted him to do this the language gave him an almost foolproof instrument and the texture of the words with their harsh sound and a certain wilful ugliness which was in accord with the spirit of ingsoc assisted the process still further
6829	so did the fact of having very few words to choose from
6830	relative to our own the newspeak vocabulary was tiny and new ways of reducing it were constantly being devised
6831	newspeak indeed differed from most all other languages in that its vocabulary grew smaller instead of larger every year
6832	each reduction was a gain since the smaller the area of choice the smaller the temptation to take thought
6833	ultimately it was hoped to make articulate speech issue from the larynx without involving the higher brain centres at all
6834	this aim was frankly admitted in the newspeak word duckspeak meaning to quack like a duck
6835	like various other words in the b vocabulary duckspeak was ambivalent in meaning
6836	provided that the opinions which were quacked out were orthodox ones it implied nothing but praise and when the times referred to one of the orators of the party as a doubleplusgood duckspeaker it was paying a warm and valued compliment
6837	the c vocabulary
6838	the c vocabulary was supplementary to the others and consisted entirely of scientific and technical terms
6839	these resembled the scientific terms in use today and were constructed from the same roots but the usual care was taken to define them rigidly and strip them of undesirable meanings
6840	they followed the same grammatical rules as the words in the other two vocabularies
6841	very few of the c words had any currency either in everyday speech or in political speech
6842	any scientific worker or technician could find all the words he needed in the list devoted to his own speciality but he seldom had more than a smattering of the words occurring in the other lists
6843	only a very few words were common to all lists and there was no vocabulary expressing the function of science as a habit of mind or a method of thought irrespective of its particular branches
6844	there was indeed no word for science any meaning that it could possibly bear being already sufficiently covered by the word ingsoc
6845	from the foregoing account it will be seen that in newspeak the expression of unorthodox opinions above a very low level was wellnigh impossible
6846	it was of course possible to utter heresies of a very crude kind a species of blasphemy
6847	it would have been possible for example to say big brother is ungood
6848	but this statement which to an orthodox ear merely conveyed a selfevident absurdity could not have been sustained by reasoned argument because the necessary words were not available
6849	ideas inimical to ingsoc could only be entertained in a vague wordless form and could only be named in very broad terms which lumped together and condemned whole groups of heresies without defining them in doing so
6850	one could in fact only use newspeak for unorthodox purposes by illegitimately translating some of the words back into oldspeak
6851	for example all mans are equal was a possible newspeak sentence but only in the same sense in which all men are redhaired is a possible oldspeak sentence
6852	it did not contain a grammatical error but it expressed a palpable untruthie
6853	that all men are of equal size weight or strength
6854	the concept of political equality no longer existed and this secondary meaning had accordingly been purged out of the word equal
6855	in when oldspeak was still the normal means of communication the danger theoretically existed that in using newspeak words one might remember their original meanings
6856	in practice it was not difficult for any person well grounded in doublethink to avoid doing this but within a couple of generations even the possibility of such a lapse would have vanished
6857	a person growing up with newspeak as his sole language would no more know that equal had once had the secondary meaning of politically equal or that free had once meant intellectually free than for instance a person who had never heard of chess would be aware of the secondary meanings attaching to queen and rook
6858	there would be many crimes and errors which it would be beyond his power to commit simply because they were nameless and therefore unimaginable
6859	and it was to be foreseen that with the passage of time the distinguishing characteristics of newspeak would become more and more pronouncedits words growing fewer and fewer their meanings more and more rigid and the chance of putting them to improper uses always diminishing
6860	when oldspeak had been once and for all superseded the last link with the past would have been severed
6861	history had already been rewritten but fragments of the literature of the past survived here and there imperfectly censored and so long as one retained ones knowledge of oldspeak it was possible to read them
6862	in the future such fragments even if they chanced to survive would be unintelligible and untranslatable
6863	it was impossible to translate any passage of oldspeak into newspeak unless it either referred to some technical process or some very simple everyday action or was already orthodox goodthinkful would be the newspeak expression in tendency
6864	in practice this meant that no book written before approximately could be translated as a whole
6865	prerevolutionary literature could only be subjected to ideological translationthat is alteration in sense as well as language
6866	take for example the wellknown passage from the declaration of independence
6867	we hold these truths to be selfevident that all men are created equal that they are endowed by their creator with certain inalienable rights that among these are life liberty and the pursuit of happiness
6868	that to secure these rights governments are instituted among men deriving their powers from the consent of the governed
6869	that whenever any form of government becomes destructive of those ends it is the right of the people to alter or abolish it and to institute new government
6870	it would have been quite impossible to render this into newspeak while keeping to the sense of the original
6871	the nearest one could come to doing so would be to swallow the whole passage up in the single word crimethink
6872	a full translation could only be an ideological translation whereby jeffersons words would be changed into a panegyric on absolute government
6873	a good deal of the literature of the past was indeed already being transformed in this way
6874	considerations of prestige made it desirable to preserve the memory of certain historical figures while at the same time bringing their achievements into line with the philosophy of ingsoc
6875	various writers such as shakespeare milton swift byron dickens and some others were therefore in process of translation when the task had been completed their original writings with all else that survived of the literature of the past would be destroyed
6876	these translations were a slow and difficult business and it was not expected that they would be finished before the first or second decade of the twentyfirst century
6877	there were also large quantities of merely utilitarian literatureindispensable technical manuals and the likethat had to be treated in the same way
6878	it was chiefly in order to allow time for the preliminary work of translation that the final adoption of newspeak had been fixed for so late a date as
6879	the end
